					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Web_search_engine&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Web search engine">Web search engine</a>)</span></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Software system for finding relevant information on the Web</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about searching the World Wide Web. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Search_engine_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Search engine (disambiguation)">Search engine (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1305433154">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px 0;overflow:hidden;width:238px;border-collapse:collapse;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em}.mw-parser-output .ambox-speedy{border-left:10px solid #b32424;background-color:#fee7e6}.mw-parser-output .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em 0;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-empty-cell{border:none;padding:0;width:1px}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-image-div{width:52px}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .ambox{display:none!important}}</style><table class="box-Tone plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Tone" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/120px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article's <b>tone or style may not reflect the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">encyclopedic tone</a> used on Wikipedia</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> See Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">guide to writing better articles</a> for suggestions.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2025</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Google_search_result_for_Magic_Flute_opera.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Google_search_result_for_Magic_Flute_opera.png/330px-Google_search_result_for_Magic_Flute_opera.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="127" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Google_search_result_for_Magic_Flute_opera.png/500px-Google_search_result_for_Magic_Flute_opera.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Google_search_result_for_Magic_Flute_opera.png/960px-Google_search_result_for_Magic_Flute_opera.png 2x" data-file-width="1302" data-file-height="550" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Google_search" class="mw-redirect" title="Google search">Google search</a> result for the phrase "magic flute opera"</figcaption></figure>
<p>A <b>search engine</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Software_system" title="Software system">software system</a> that provides <a href="/wiki/Hyperlink" title="Hyperlink">hyperlinks</a> to <a href="/wiki/Web_page" title="Web page">web pages</a>, and other relevant information on <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">the Web</a> in response to a user's <a href="/wiki/Web_query" title="Web query">query</a>. The user enters a query in a <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browser</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">mobile app</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Search_engine_results_page" title="Search engine results page">search results</a> are typically presented as a list of hyperlinks accompanied by textual summaries and images. Users also have the option of limiting a search to specific types of results, such as <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">images</a>, <a href="/wiki/Video" title="Video">videos</a>, or <a href="/wiki/News" title="News">news</a>.
</p><p>For a search provider, its <a href="/wiki/Software_engine" title="Software engine">engine</a> is part of a <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> system that can encompass many <a href="/wiki/Data_center" title="Data center">data centers</a> throughout the world. The speed and accuracy of an engine's response to a query are based on a complex system of <a href="/wiki/Search_engine_indexing" title="Search engine indexing">indexing</a> that is continuously updated by automated <a href="/wiki/Web_crawler" title="Web crawler">web crawlers</a>. This can include <a href="/wiki/Data_mining" title="Data mining">data mining</a> the <a href="/wiki/Computer_file" title="Computer file">files</a> and <a href="/wiki/Database" title="Database">databases</a> stored on <a href="/wiki/Web_server" title="Web server">web servers</a>, although some content is <a href="/wiki/Deep_web" title="Deep web">not accessible</a> to crawlers.
</p><p>There have been many search engines since the dawn of the Web in the 1990s; however, <a href="/wiki/Google_Search" title="Google Search">Google Search</a> became the dominant one in the 2000s and has remained so. As of May 2025, according to StatCounter, Google holds approximately 89–90 % of the worldwide search share, with competitors trailing far behind: <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Bing" title="Microsoft Bing">Bing</a> (~4 %), <a href="/wiki/Yandex_Search" title="Yandex Search">Yandex</a> (~2.5 %), <a href="/wiki/Yahoo" title="Yahoo">Yahoo</a>! (~1.3 %), <a href="/wiki/DuckDuckGo" title="DuckDuckGo">DuckDuckGo</a> (~0.8 %), and <a href="/wiki/Baidu" title="Baidu">Baidu</a> (~0.7 %).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-StatCounterMay2025&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StatCounterMay2025-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Notably, this marks the first time in over a decade that Google's share has fallen below the 90% threshold. The business of <a href="/wiki/Website" title="Website">websites</a> improving their visibility in <a href="/wiki/Search_results" class="mw-redirect" title="Search results">search results</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Search_engine_marketing" title="Search engine marketing">marketing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Search_engine_optimization" title="Search engine optimization">optimization</a>, has thus largely focused on Google.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_web_search_engines" title="Timeline of web search engines">Timeline of web search engines</a></div>
<table class="wikitable sortable" style="margin-left:1em; float:right; width:25em">
<caption>Timeline (<a href="/wiki/List_of_search_engines" title="List of search engines">full list</a>)
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th scope="col">Year
</th>
<th scope="col">Engine
</th>
<th style="width: 13em;" scope="col">Current status
</th></tr>
<tr>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pre-1990s">Pre-1990s</h3></div>
<p>In 1945, <a href="/wiki/Vannevar_Bush" title="Vannevar Bush">Vannevar Bush</a> described an information retrieval system that would allow a user to access a great expanse of information, all at a single desk, which he called a <a href="/wiki/Memex" title="Memex">memex</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He described this system in an article titled "<a href="/wiki/As_We_May_Think" title="As We May Think">As We May Think</a>" in <a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic"><i>The Atlantic Monthly</i></a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Memex" title="Memex">memex</a> was intended to give a user the capability to overcome the ever-increasing difficulty of locating information in ever-growing centralized indices of scientific work. Vannevar Bush envisioned libraries of research with connected annotations, which are similar to modern <a href="/wiki/Hyperlink" title="Hyperlink">hyperlinks</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Link_analysis" title="Link analysis">Link analysis</a> eventually became a crucial component of search engines through algorithms such as <a href="/wiki/Hyper_Search" title="Hyper Search">Hyper Search</a> and <a href="/wiki/PageRank" title="PageRank">PageRank</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-AnatomyOfSearch&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AnatomyOfSearch-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="1990s:_Birth_of_search_engines">1990s: Birth of search engines</h3></div>
<p>The first internet search engines predate the debut of the Web in December 1990: <a href="/wiki/WHOIS" title="WHOIS">WHOIS</a> user search dates back to 1982,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc812&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc812-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Knowbot_Information_Service" title="Knowbot Information Service">Knowbot Information Service</a> multi-network user search was first implemented in 1989.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first well documented search engine that searched content files, namely <a href="/wiki/FTP" class="mw-redirect" title="FTP">FTP</a> files, was <a href="/wiki/Archie_search_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Archie search engine">Archie</a>, which debuted on 10 September 1990.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to September 1993, the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a> was entirely indexed by hand. There was a list of <a href="/wiki/Webserver" class="mw-redirect" title="Webserver">webservers</a> edited by <a href="/wiki/Tim_Berners-Lee" title="Tim Berners-Lee">Tim Berners-Lee</a> and hosted on the <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a> <a href="/wiki/Web_server" title="Web server">webserver</a>. One snapshot of the list in 1992 remains,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but as more and more web servers went online the central list could no longer keep up. On the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Supercomputing_Applications" title="National Center for Supercomputing Applications">NCSA</a> site, new servers were announced under the title "What's New!".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The first tool used for searching content (as opposed to users) on the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> was <a href="/wiki/Archie_search_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Archie search engine">Archie</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-LeidenUnivSE&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeidenUnivSE-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The name stands for "archive" without the "v".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2020/09/21pcmag&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2020/09/21pcmag-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was created by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Emtage" title="Alan Emtage">Alan Emtage</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2020/09/21pcmag&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2020/09/21pcmag-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> student at <a href="/wiki/McGill_University" title="McGill University">McGill University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Montreal,_Quebec" class="mw-redirect" title="Montreal, Quebec">Montreal, Quebec</a>, Canada. The program downloaded the directory listings of all the files located on public anonymous FTP (<a href="/wiki/File_Transfer_Protocol" title="File Transfer Protocol">File Transfer Protocol</a>) sites, creating a searchable <a href="/wiki/Database" title="Database">database</a> of file names; however, <a href="/wiki/Archie_search_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Archie search engine">Archie Search Engine</a> did not index the contents of these sites since the amount of data was so limited it could be readily searched manually.
</p><p>The rise of <a href="/wiki/Gopher_(protocol)" title="Gopher (protocol)">Gopher</a> (created in 1991 by <a href="/wiki/Mark_McCahill" class="mw-redirect" title="Mark McCahill">Mark McCahill</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Minnesota" title="University of Minnesota">University of Minnesota</a>) led to two new search programs, <a href="/wiki/Veronica_(search_engine)" title="Veronica (search engine)">Veronica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jughead_(search_engine)" title="Jughead (search engine)">Jughead</a>. Like Archie, they searched the file names and titles stored in Gopher index systems. Veronica (Very Easy Rodent-Oriented Net-wide Index to Computerized Archives) provided a keyword search of most Gopher menu titles in the entire Gopher listings. Jughead (Jonzy's Universal Gopher Hierarchy Excavation And Display) was a tool for obtaining menu information from specific Gopher servers. While the name of the search engine "<a href="/wiki/Archie_search_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Archie search engine">Archie Search Engine</a>" was not a reference to the <a href="/wiki/Archie_Comics" title="Archie Comics">Archie comic book</a> series, "<a href="/wiki/Veronica_Lodge" title="Veronica Lodge">Veronica</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Jughead_Jones" title="Jughead Jones">Jughead</a>" are characters in the series, thus referencing their predecessor.
</p><p>In the summer of 1993, no search engine existed for the web, though numerous specialized catalogs were maintained by hand. <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Nierstrasz" title="Oscar Nierstrasz">Oscar Nierstrasz</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Geneva" title="University of Geneva">University of Geneva</a> wrote a series of <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a> scripts that periodically mirrored these pages and rewrote them into a standard format. This formed the basis for <a href="/wiki/W3Catalog" title="W3Catalog">W3Catalog</a>, the web's first primitive search engine, released on September 2, 1993.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Announcement&#95;html&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Announcement_html-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In June 1993, Matthew Gray, then at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a>, produced what was probably the first <a href="/wiki/Web_robot" class="mw-redirect" title="Web robot">web robot</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a>-based <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web_Wanderer" title="World Wide Web Wanderer">World Wide Web Wanderer</a>, and used it to generate an index called "Wandex". The purpose of the Wanderer was to measure the size of the World Wide Web, which it did until late 1995. The web's second search engine <a href="/wiki/Aliweb" class="mw-redirect" title="Aliweb">Aliweb</a> appeared in November 1993. Aliweb did not use a <a href="/wiki/Web_robot" class="mw-redirect" title="Web robot">web robot</a>, but instead depended on being notified by <a href="/wiki/Webmaster" title="Webmaster">website administrators</a> of the existence at each site of an index file in a particular format.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/JumpStation" title="JumpStation">JumpStation</a> (created in December 1993<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Jonathon_Fletcher" class="mw-redirect" title="Jonathon Fletcher">Jonathon Fletcher</a>) used a <a href="/wiki/Web_crawler" title="Web crawler">web robot</a> to find web pages and to build its index, and used a <a href="/wiki/Web_form" class="mw-redirect" title="Web form">web form</a> as the interface to its query program. It was thus the first <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">WWW</a> resource-discovery tool to combine the three essential features of a web search engine (crawling, indexing, and searching) as described below. Because of the limited resources available on the platform it ran on, its indexing and hence searching were limited to the titles and headings found in the <a href="/wiki/Web_page" title="Web page">web pages</a> the crawler encountered.
</p><p>One of the first "all text" crawler-based search engines was <a href="/wiki/WebCrawler" title="WebCrawler">WebCrawler</a>, which came out in 1994. Unlike its predecessors, it allowed users to search for any word in any <a href="/wiki/Web_page" title="Web page">web page</a>, which has become the standard for all major search engines since. It was also the search engine that was widely known by the public. Also, in 1994, <a href="/wiki/Lycos" title="Lycos">Lycos</a> (which started at <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">Carnegie Mellon University</a>) was launched and became a major commercial endeavor.
</p><p>The first popular search engine on the Web was <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_Search" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo! Search">Yahoo! Search</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first product from <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a>, founded by <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Yang" title="Jerry Yang">Jerry Yang</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Filo" title="David Filo">David Filo</a> in January 1994, was a <a href="/wiki/Web_directory" title="Web directory">Web directory</a> called <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_Directory" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo! Directory">Yahoo! Directory</a>. In 1995, a search function was added, allowing users to search Yahoo! Directory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It became one of the most popular ways for people to find web pages of interest, but its search function operated on its web directory, rather than its full-text copies of web pages.
</p><p>Soon after, a number of search engines appeared and vied for popularity. These included <a href="/wiki/Magellan_(search_engine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Magellan (search engine)">Magellan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Excite_(web_portal)" title="Excite (web portal)">Excite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infoseek" title="Infoseek">Infoseek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inktomi_(company)" class="mw-redirect" title="Inktomi (company)">Inktomi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Light_Group" title="Northern Light Group">Northern Light</a>, and <a href="/wiki/AltaVista" title="AltaVista">AltaVista</a>. Information seekers could also browse the directory instead of doing a keyword-based search.
</p><p>In 1996, <a href="/wiki/Robin_Li" title="Robin Li">Robin Li</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/RankDex" class="mw-redirect" title="RankDex">RankDex</a> site-scoring <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a> for search engines results page ranking<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-rankdex&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rankdex-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and received a US patent for the technology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was the first search engine that used <a href="/wiki/Hyperlink" title="Hyperlink">hyperlinks</a> to measure the quality of websites it was indexing,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> predating the very similar algorithm patent filed by <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> two years later in 1998.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a> referenced Li's work in some of his U.S. patents for PageRank.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patent&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patent-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Li later used his RankDex technology for the <a href="/wiki/Baidu" title="Baidu">Baidu</a> search engine, which was founded by him in China and launched in 2000.
</p><p>In 1996, <a href="/wiki/Netscape" title="Netscape">Netscape</a> was looking to give a single search engine an exclusive deal as the featured search engine on Netscape's web browser. There was so much interest that instead, Netscape struck deals with five of the major search engines: for $5 million a year, each search engine would be in rotation on the Netscape search engine page. The five engines were Yahoo!, Magellan, Lycos, Infoseek, and Excite.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> adopted the idea of selling search terms in 1998 from a small search engine company named <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_Search_Marketing" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo! Search Marketing">goto.com</a>. This move had a significant effect on the search engine business, which went from struggling to one of the most profitable businesses in the Internet.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Search engines were also known as some of the brightest stars in the Internet investing frenzy that occurred in the late 1990s.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several companies entered the market spectacularly, receiving record gains during their <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offerings</a>. Some have taken down their public search engine and are marketing enterprise-only editions, such as Northern Light. Many search engine companies were caught up in the <a href="/wiki/Dot-com_bubble" title="Dot-com bubble">dot-com bubble</a>, a speculation-driven market boom that peaked in March 2000.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="2000s–present:_Post_dot-com_bubble"><span id="2000s.E2.80.93present:_Post_dot-com_bubble"></span>2000s–present: Post dot-com bubble</h3></div>
<p>Around 2000, <a href="/wiki/Google_Search" title="Google Search">Google's search engine</a> rose to prominence.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The company achieved better results for many searches with an algorithm called <a href="/wiki/PageRank" title="PageRank">PageRank</a>, as was explained in the paper <i>Anatomy of a Search Engine</i> written by <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Brin" title="Sergey Brin">Sergey Brin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a>, the later founders of Google.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AnatomyOfSearch&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AnatomyOfSearch-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Iterative_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Iterative algorithm">iterative algorithm</a> ranks web pages based on the number and PageRank of other web sites and pages that link there, on the premise that good or desirable pages are linked to more than others. Larry Page's patent for PageRank cites <a href="/wiki/Robin_Li" title="Robin Li">Robin Li</a>'s earlier <a href="/wiki/RankDex" class="mw-redirect" title="RankDex">RankDex</a> patent as an influence.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patent&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patent-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-rankdex&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rankdex-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Google also maintained a minimalist interface to its search engine. In contrast, many of its competitors embedded a search engine in a <a href="/wiki/Web_portal" title="Web portal">web portal</a>. In fact, the Google search engine became so popular that spoof engines emerged such as <a href="/wiki/Mystery_Seeker" title="Mystery Seeker">Mystery Seeker</a>.
</p><p>By 2000, <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a> was providing search services based on Inktomi's search engine. Yahoo! acquired Inktomi in 2002, and <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_Native" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo! Native">Overture</a> (which owned <a href="/wiki/AlltheWeb" title="AlltheWeb">AlltheWeb</a> and AltaVista) in 2003. Yahoo! switched to Google's search engine until 2004, when it launched its own search engine based on the combined technologies of its acquisitions.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> first launched MSN Search in the fall of 1998 using search results from Inktomi. In early 1999, the site began to display listings from <a href="/wiki/Looksmart" class="mw-redirect" title="Looksmart">Looksmart</a>, blended with results from Inktomi. For a short time in 1999, MSN Search used results from AltaVista instead. In 2004, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> began a transition to its own search technology, powered by its own <a href="/wiki/Web_crawler" title="Web crawler">web crawler</a> (called <a href="/wiki/Msnbot" title="Msnbot">msnbot</a>).
</p><p>Microsoft's rebranded search engine, <a href="/wiki/Bing_(search_engine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bing (search engine)">Bing</a>, was launched on June 1, 2009. On July 29, 2009, Yahoo! and Microsoft finalized a deal in which <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_Search" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo! Search">Yahoo! Search</a> would be powered by Microsoft Bing technology.
</p><p>As of 2019,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Search_engine&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> active search engine crawlers include those of <a href="/wiki/Baidu" title="Baidu">Baidu</a>, Bing, <a href="/wiki/Brave_Search" title="Brave Search">Brave</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Google, <a href="/wiki/DuckDuckGo" title="DuckDuckGo">DuckDuckGo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gigablast" title="Gigablast">Gigablast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mojeek" title="Mojeek">Mojeek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sogou" title="Sogou">Sogou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yandex" title="Yandex">Yandex</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Approach">Approach</h2></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Workings"></span>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Search_engine_technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Search engine technology">Search engine technology</a></div>
<p>A search engine maintains the following processes in near real time:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Web_crawling" class="mw-redirect" title="Web crawling">Web crawling</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Index_(search_engine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Index (search engine)">Indexing</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Web_search_query" class="mw-redirect" title="Web search query">Searching</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jawadekar2011&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jawadekar2011-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Web search engines get their information by <a href="/wiki/Web_crawler" title="Web crawler">web crawling</a> from site to site. The "spider" checks for the standard filename <i><a href="/wiki/Robots.txt" title="Robots.txt">robots.txt</a></i>, addressed to it. The robots.txt file contains directives for search spiders, telling it which pages to crawl and which pages not to crawl. After checking for robots.txt and either finding it or not, the spider sends certain information back to be <a href="/wiki/Search_engine_indexing" title="Search engine indexing">indexed</a> depending on many factors, such as the titles, page content, <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cascading_Style_Sheets" class="mw-redirect" title="Cascading Style Sheets">Cascading Style Sheets</a> (CSS), headings, or its <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">metadata</a> in HTML <a href="/wiki/Meta_tags" class="mw-redirect" title="Meta tags">meta tags</a>. After a certain number of pages crawled, amount of data indexed, or time spent on the website, the spider stops crawling and moves on. "[N]o web crawler may actually crawl the entire reachable web. Due to infinite websites, spider traps, spam, and other exigencies of the real web, crawlers instead apply a crawl policy to determine when the crawling of a site should be deemed sufficient. Some websites are crawled exhaustively, while others are crawled only partially".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Indexing means associating words and other definable tokens found on web pages to their domain names and <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">HTML</a>-based fields. The associations are stored in a public database and accessible through web search queries. A query from a user can be a single word, multiple words or a sentence. The index helps find information relating to the query as quickly as possible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jawadekar2011&#95;37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jawadekar2011-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some of the techniques for indexing, and <a href="/wiki/Search_engine_cache" title="Search engine cache">caching</a> are trade secrets, whereas web crawling is a straightforward process of visiting all sites on a systematic basis.
</p><p>Between visits by the <i>spider</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Cache_(computing)" title="Cache (computing)">cached</a> version of the page (some or all the content needed to render it) stored in the search engine working memory is quickly sent to an inquirer. If a visit is overdue, the search engine can just act as a <a href="/wiki/Web_proxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Web proxy">web proxy</a> instead. In this case, the page may differ from the search terms indexed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jawadekar2011&#95;37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jawadekar2011-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The cached page holds the appearance of the version whose words were previously indexed, so a cached version of a page can be useful to the website when the actual page has been lost, but this problem is also considered a mild form of <a href="/wiki/Linkrot" class="mw-redirect" title="Linkrot">linkrot</a>.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:WebCrawlerArchitecture.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/WebCrawlerArchitecture.svg/250px-WebCrawlerArchitecture.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="191" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/WebCrawlerArchitecture.svg/500px-WebCrawlerArchitecture.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="382" /></a><figcaption>High-level architecture of a standard Web crawler</figcaption></figure>
<p>Typically, when a user enters a <a href="/wiki/Web_search_query" class="mw-redirect" title="Web search query">query</a> into a search engine it is a few <a href="/wiki/Keyword_(Internet_search)" class="mw-redirect" title="Keyword (Internet search)">keywords</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jansen2000&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jansen2000-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Inverted_index" title="Inverted index">index</a> already has the names of the sites containing the keywords, and these are instantly obtained from the index. The real processing load is in generating the web pages that are the search results list: Every page in the entire list must be <a href="/wiki/Weighting" class="mw-redirect" title="Weighting">weighted</a> according to information in the indexes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jawadekar2011&#95;37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jawadekar2011-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Then the top search result item requires the lookup, reconstruction, and markup of the <i><a href="/wiki/Snippet_(programming)" title="Snippet (programming)">snippets</a></i> showing the context of the keywords matched. These are only part of the processing each search results web page requires, and further pages (next to the top) require more of this post-processing.
</p><p>Beyond simple keyword lookups, search engines offer their own <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">GUI</a>- or command-driven operators and search parameters to refine the search results. These provide the necessary controls for the user engaged in the feedback loop users create by <i>filtering</i> and <i>weighting</i> while refining the search results, given the initial pages of the first search results. For example, from 2007 the Google.com search engine has allowed one to <i>filter</i> by date by clicking "Show search tools" in the leftmost column of the initial search results page, and then selecting the desired date range.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is also possible to <i>weight</i> by date because each page has a modification time. Most search engines support the use of the <a href="/wiki/Boolean_operator_(computer_programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Boolean operator (computer programming)">Boolean operators</a> AND, OR and NOT to help end users refine the <a href="/wiki/Web_search_query" class="mw-redirect" title="Web search query">search query</a>. Boolean operators are for literal searches that allow the user to refine and extend the terms of the search. The engine looks for the words or phrases exactly as entered. Some search engines provide an advanced feature called <a href="/wiki/Proximity_search_(text)" title="Proximity search (text)">proximity search</a>, which allows users to define the distance between keywords.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jawadekar2011&#95;37-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jawadekar2011-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is also <a href="/wiki/Concept_search" title="Concept search">concept-based searching</a> where the research involves using statistical analysis on pages containing the words or phrases the user searches for.
</p><p>The usefulness of a search engine depends on the <a href="/wiki/Relevance_(information_retrieval)" title="Relevance (information retrieval)">relevance</a> of the <i>result set</i> it gives back. While there may be millions of web pages that include a particular word or phrase, some pages may be more relevant, popular, or authoritative than others. Most search engines employ methods to <a href="/wiki/Rank_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Rank order">rank</a> the results to provide the "best" results first. How a search engine decides which pages are the best matches, and what order the results should be shown in, varies widely from one engine to another.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jawadekar2011&#95;37-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jawadekar2011-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The methods also change over time as Internet usage changes and new techniques evolve. There are two main types of search engine that have evolved: one is a system of predefined and hierarchically ordered keywords that humans have programmed extensively. The other is a system that generates an "<a href="/wiki/Inverted_index" title="Inverted index">inverted index</a>" by analyzing texts it locates. This first form relies much more heavily on the computer itself to do the bulk of the work.
</p><p>Most Web search engines are commercial ventures supported by <a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">advertising</a> revenue and thus some of them allow advertisers to <a href="/wiki/Paid_inclusion" title="Paid inclusion">have their listings ranked higher</a> in search results for a fee. Search engines that do not accept money for their search results make money by running <a href="/wiki/Contextual_advertising" title="Contextual advertising">search related ads</a> alongside the regular search engine results. The search engines make money every time someone clicks on one of these ads.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Local_search">Local search</h3></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Local_search_(Internet)" title="Local search (Internet)">Local search</a> is the process that optimizes the efforts of local businesses. They focus on ensuring consistent search results. It is important because many people determine where they plan to go and what to buy based on their searches.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Market_share">Market share</h2></div>
<p>As of January&#160;2022,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Search_engine&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_Search" title="Google Search">Google</a> is by far the world's most used search engine, with a market share of 90%, and the world's other most used search engines were <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Bing" title="Microsoft Bing">Bing</a> at 4%, <a href="/wiki/Yandex_Search" title="Yandex Search">Yandex</a> at 2%, <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_Search" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo! Search">Yahoo!</a> at 1%. Other search engines not listed have less than a 3% market share.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NMS&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NMS-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2024, Google's dominance was ruled an illegal monopoly in a case brought by the US Department of Justice.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Russia_and_East_Asia">Russia and East Asia</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1305433154" /><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/60px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png" decoding="async" width="42" height="34" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/120px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2023</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>As of late 2023 and early 2024, search engine market shares in Russia and East Asia have remained relatively stable but with some notable shifts due to geopolitical and technological developments.
</p><p>In Russia, Yandex continues to dominate the search engine market with a share of approximately 70.7%, while Google holds around 23.3%.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Yandex also remains a key player in localized services including navigation, ride-hailing, and e-commerce, strengthening its ecosystem.
</p><p>In China, Baidu remains the leading search engine with a market share of about 59.3% as of early 2024. Other domestic engines such as Sogou and 360 Search hold smaller shares. Google remains inaccessible in mainland China due to long-standing censorship issues, having exited the Chinese market in 2010 following disputes over censorship and cybersecurity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bing, Microsoft's search engine, has maintained a niche presence in China with a market share around 13.6%, making it one of the few foreign search engines operating under local regulatory constraints.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In South Korea, Naver continues to lead the domestic market, with a market share of 59.8%, followed by Google at 35.4% as of Q4 2023.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Naver’s strength lies in its localized services and integration with Korean content platforms.
</p><p>In Japan, Google Japan currently holds the largest market share (around 76.2%), while Yahoo! Japan, operated by Z Holdings (a SoftBank and Naver joint venture), retains about 15.8% market share.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In Taiwan, Google is the predominant search engine, commanding over 93% of the market, with Yahoo! Taiwan and Bing trailing far behind.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Search_engine_bias">Search engine bias</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Algorithmic_bias" title="Algorithmic bias">Algorithmic bias</a></div>
<p>Although search engines are programmed to rank websites based on some combination of their popularity and relevancy, empirical studies indicate various political, economic, and social biases in the information they provide<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-vaughan-thelwall&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vaughan-thelwall-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the underlying assumptions about the technology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These biases can be a direct result of economic and commercial processes (e.g., companies that advertise with a search engine can become also more popular in its <a href="/wiki/Organic_search" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic search">organic search</a> results), and political processes (e.g., the removal of search results to comply with local laws).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, Google will not surface certain <a href="/wiki/Neo-Nazism" title="Neo-Nazism">neo-Nazi</a> websites in France and Germany, where <a href="/wiki/Holocaust_denial" title="Holocaust denial">Holocaust denial</a> is illegal.
</p><p>Biases can also be a result of social processes, as search engine algorithms are frequently designed to exclude non-normative viewpoints in favor of more "popular" results.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Indexing algorithms of major search engines skew towards coverage of U.S.-based sites, rather than websites from non-U.S. countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-vaughan-thelwall&#95;53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vaughan-thelwall-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Google_Bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="Google Bombing">Google Bombing</a> is one example of an attempt to manipulate search results for political, social or commercial reasons.
</p><p>Several scholars have studied the cultural changes triggered by search engines,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the representation of certain controversial topics in their results, such as <a href="/wiki/Terrorism_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Terrorism in Ireland">terrorism in Ireland</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_denial" title="Climate change denial">climate change denial</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_theory" title="Conspiracy theory">conspiracy theories</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Customized_results_and_filter_bubbles">Customized results and filter bubbles</h2></div>
<p>There has been concern raised that search engines such as Google and Bing provide customized results based on the user's activity history, leading to what has been termed echo chambers or <a href="/wiki/Filter_bubble" title="Filter bubble">filter bubbles</a> by <a href="/wiki/Eli_Pariser" title="Eli Pariser">Eli Pariser</a> in 2011.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The argument is that search engines and social media platforms use <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithms</a> to selectively guess what information a user would like to see, based on information about the user (such as location, past click behavior and search history). As a result, websites tend to show only information that agrees with the user's past viewpoint. According to <a href="/wiki/Eli_Pariser" title="Eli Pariser">Eli Pariser</a> users get less exposure to conflicting viewpoints and are isolated intellectually in their own informational bubble. Since this problem has been identified, competing search engines have emerged that seek to avoid this problem by not tracking or "bubbling" users, such as <a href="/wiki/DuckDuckGo" title="DuckDuckGo">DuckDuckGo</a>. However many scholars have questioned Pariser's view, finding that there is little evidence for the filter bubble.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On the contrary, a number of studies trying to verify the existence of filter bubbles have found only minor levels of personalization in search,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that most people encounter a range of views when browsing online, and that Google news tends to promote mainstream established news outlets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Religious_search_engines">Religious search engines</h2></div>
<p>The global growth of the Internet and electronic media in the <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> world during the last decade has encouraged Islamic adherents in <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">the Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Asian sub-continent</a>, to attempt their own search engines, their own filtered search portals that would enable users to perform <a href="/wiki/Content-control_software" class="mw-redirect" title="Content-control software">safe searches</a>. More than usual <i>safe search</i> filters, these Islamic web portals categorizing websites into being either "<a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Haram" title="Haram">haram</a>", based on interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia law</a>. <a href="/wiki/ImHalal" title="ImHalal">ImHalal</a> came online in September 2011. <a href="/wiki/Halalgoogling" title="Halalgoogling">Halalgoogling</a> came online in July 2013. These use <a href="/wiki/Haram" title="Haram">haram</a> filters on the collections from <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bing_(search_engine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bing (search engine)">Bing</a> (and others).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>While lack of investment and slow pace in technologies in the Muslim world has hindered progress and thwarted success of an Islamic search engine, targeting as the main consumers Islamic adherents, projects like <a href="/wiki/Muxlim" title="Muxlim">Muxlim</a> (a Muslim lifestyle site) received millions of dollars from investors like Rite Internet Ventures, and it also faltered. Other religion-oriented search engines are Jewogle, the Jewish version of Google,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Christian search engine SeekFind.org. SeekFind filters sites that attack or degrade their faith.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Search_engine_submission">Search engine submission</h2></div>
<p>Web search engine submission is a process in which a webmaster submits a website directly to a search engine. While search engine submission is sometimes presented as a way to promote a website, it generally is not necessary because the major search engines use web crawlers that will eventually find most web sites on the Internet without assistance. They can either submit one web page at a time, or they can submit the entire site using a <a href="/wiki/Sitemap" class="mw-redirect" title="Sitemap">sitemap</a>, but it is normally only necessary to submit the <a href="/wiki/Home_page" title="Home page">home page</a> of a web site as search engines are able to crawl a well designed website. There are two remaining reasons to submit a web site or web page to a search engine: to add an entirely new web site without waiting for a search engine to discover it, and to have a web site's record updated after a substantial redesign.
</p><p>Some search engine submission software not only submits websites to multiple search engines, but also adds links to websites from their own pages. This could appear helpful in increasing a website's <a href="/wiki/Ranking_(information_retrieval)" title="Ranking (information retrieval)">ranking</a>, because external links are one of the most important factors determining a website's ranking. However, John Mueller of <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> has stated that this "can lead to a tremendous number of unnatural links for your site" with a negative impact on site ranking.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CanBeHarmful&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CanBeHarmful-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Comparison_to_social_bookmarking">Comparison to social bookmarking</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Social_media_optimization" title="Social media optimization">Social media optimization</a></div>
<p>In comparison to search engines, a social bookmarking system has several advantages over traditional automated resource location and classification software, such as <a class="mw-selflink selflink">search engine</a> <a href="/wiki/Web_crawler" title="Web crawler">spiders</a>. All tag-based classification of Internet resources (such as web sites) is done by human beings, who understand the content of the resource, as opposed to software, which algorithmically attempts to determine the meaning and quality of a resource. Also, people can find and <a href="/wiki/Bookmark_(digital)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bookmark (digital)">bookmark web pages</a> that have not yet been noticed or indexed by web spiders.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-acm-bookmarking&#95;70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acm-bookmarking-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, a social bookmarking system can rank a resource based on how many times it has been bookmarked by users, which may be a more useful <a href="/wiki/Figure_of_merit" title="Figure of merit">metric</a> for <a href="/wiki/End-user" class="mw-redirect" title="End-user">end-users</a> than systems that rank resources based on the number of external links pointing to it.  However, both types of ranking are vulnerable to fraud, (see <a href="/wiki/Gaming_the_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaming the system">Gaming the system</a>), and both need technical countermeasures to try to deal with this.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Technology">Technology</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1305433154" /><table class="box-Cleanup&#95;split plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/60px-Split-arrows.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="17" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/120px-Split-arrows.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="60" data-file-height="20" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article or section <b>may need to be cleaned up or summarized.</b> It has been split from/to <i><a href="/wiki/Search_engine_technology#Web_search_engines" class="mw-redirect" title="Search engine technology">Search engine technology#Web search engines</a></i>.</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Archie">Archie</h3></div>
<p>The first web search engine was <a href="/wiki/Archie_(search_engine)" title="Archie (search engine)">Archie</a>, created in 1990<sup id="cite&#95;ref-intelligent-technologies&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-intelligent-technologies-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Emtage" title="Alan Emtage">Alan Emtage</a>, a student at <a href="/wiki/McGill_University" title="McGill University">McGill University</a> in Montreal.
</p><p>The primary method of storing and retrieving files was via the <a href="/wiki/File_Transfer_Protocol" title="File Transfer Protocol">File Transfer Protocol</a> (FTP). This was (and still is) a system that specified a common way for computers to exchange files over the Internet. It works like this: An administrator decides that they want to make files available from their computer. They set up a program on their computer, called an FTP server. When someone on the Internet wants to retrieve a file from this computer, they connect to it via another program called an FTP client. Any FTP client program can connect with any FTP server program as long as the client and server programs both fully follow the specifications set forth in the FTP protocol.
</p><p>Initially, anyone who wanted to share a file had to set up an FTP server in order to make the file available to others. Later, "anonymous" FTP sites became repositories for files, allowing all users to post and retrieve them.
</p><p>Even with archive sites, many important files were still scattered on small FTP servers. These files could be located only by the Internet equivalent of word of mouth: Somebody would post an e-mail to a message list or a discussion forum announcing the availability of a file.
</p><p>Archie changed all that. It combined a script-based data gatherer, which fetched site listings of anonymous FTP files, with a regular expression matcher for retrieving file names matching a user query. (4) In other words, Archie's gatherer scoured FTP sites across the Internet and indexed all of the files it found. Its regular expression matcher provided users with access to its database.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wileyhistory&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wileyhistory-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Veronica">Veronica</h3></div>
<p>In 1993, the University of Nevada System Computing Services group developed <a href="/wiki/Veronica_(search_engine)" title="Veronica (search engine)">Veronica</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-intelligent-technologies&#95;71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-intelligent-technologies-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was created as a type of searching device similar to Archie but for Gopher files. Another Gopher search service, called Jughead, appeared a little later, probably for the sole purpose of rounding out the comic-strip triumvirate. Jughead is an acronym for Jonzy's Universal Gopher Hierarchy Excavation and Display, although, like Veronica, it is probably safe to assume that the creator backed into the acronym. Jughead's functionality was pretty much identical to Veronica's, although it appears to be a little rougher around the edges.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wileyhistory&#95;72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wileyhistory-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="The_Lone_Wanderer">The Lone Wanderer</h3></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web_Wanderer" title="World Wide Web Wanderer">World Wide Web Wanderer</a>, developed by Matthew Gray in 1993<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> was the first robot on the Web and was designed to track the Web's growth. Initially, the Wanderer counted only Web servers, but shortly after its introduction, it started to capture URLs as it went along. The database of captured URLs became the Wandex, the first web database.
</p><p>Matthew Gray's Wanderer created quite a controversy at the time, partially because early versions of the software ran rampant through the Net and caused a noticeable netwide performance degradation. This degradation occurred because the Wanderer would access the same page hundreds of times a day. The Wanderer soon amended its ways, but the controversy over whether robots were good or bad for the Internet remained.
</p><p>In response to the Wanderer, Martijn Koster created Archie-Like Indexing of the Web, or ALIWEB, in October 1993. As the name implies, ALIWEB was the HTTP equivalent of Archie, and because of this, it is still unique in many ways.
</p><p>ALIWEB does not have a web-searching robot. Instead, webmasters of participating sites post their own index information for each page they want listed. The advantage to this method is that users get to describe their own site, and a robot does not run about eating up Net bandwidth. The disadvantages of ALIWEB are more of a problem today. The primary disadvantage is that a special indexing file must be submitted. Most users do not understand how to create such a file, and therefore they do not submit their pages. This leads to a relatively small database, which meant that users are less likely to search ALIWEB than one of the large bot-based sites. This Catch-22 has been somewhat offset by incorporating other databases into the ALIWEB search, but it still does not have the mass appeal of search engines such as Yahoo! or Lycos.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wileyhistory&#95;72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wileyhistory-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Excite">Excite</h3></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Excite_(web_portal)" title="Excite (web portal)">Excite</a>, initially called Architext, was started by six Stanford undergraduates in February 1993. Their idea was to use statistical analysis of word relationships in order to provide more efficient searches through the large amount of information on the Internet. Their project was fully funded by mid-1993. Once funding was secured. they released a version of their search software for webmasters to use on their own web sites. At the time, the software was called Architext, but it now goes by the name of Excite for Web Servers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wileyhistory&#95;72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wileyhistory-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Excite was the first serious commercial search engine which launched in 1995.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was developed in Stanford and was purchased for $6.5 billion by @Home. In 2001 Excite and @Home went bankrupt and <a href="/wiki/InfoSpace" title="InfoSpace">InfoSpace</a> bought Excite for $10 million.
</p><p>Some of the first analysis of web searching was conducted on search logs from Excite<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jansen2000&#95;39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jansen2000-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Yahoo!"><span id="Yahoo.21"></span>Yahoo!</h3></div>
<p>In April 1994, two Stanford University Ph.D. candidates, <a href="/wiki/David_Filo" title="David Filo">David Filo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Yang_(entrepreneur)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jerry Yang (entrepreneur)">Jerry Yang</a>, created some pages that became rather popular. They called the collection of pages <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a> Their official explanation for the name choice was that they considered themselves to be a pair of yahoos.
</p><p>As the number of links grew and their pages began to receive thousands of hits a day, the team created ways to better organize the data. In order to aid in data retrieval, Yahoo! (www.yahoo.com) became a searchable directory. The search feature was a simple database search engine. Because Yahoo! entries were entered and categorized manually, Yahoo! was not really classified as a search engine. Instead, it was generally considered to be a searchable directory. Yahoo! has since automated some aspects of the gathering and classification process, blurring the distinction between engine and directory.
</p><p>The Wanderer captured only URLs, which made it difficult to find things that were not explicitly described by their URL. Because URLs are rather cryptic to begin with, this did not help the average user. Searching Yahoo! or the Galaxy was much more effective because they contained additional descriptive information about the indexed sites.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Lycos">Lycos</h3></div>
<p>At Carnegie Mellon University during July 1994, Michael Mauldin, on leave from CMU, developed the <a href="/wiki/Lycos" title="Lycos">Lycos</a> search engine.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Types_of_web_search_engines">Types of web search engines</h3></div>
<p>Search engines on the web are sites enriched with facility to search the content stored on other sites. There is difference in the way various search engines work, but they all perform three basic tasks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Finding and selecting full or partial content based on the keywords provided.</li>
<li>Maintaining index of the content and referencing to the location they find</li>
<li>Allowing users to look for words or combinations of words found in that index.</li></ol>
<p>The process begins when a user enters a query statement into the system through the interface provided.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Type
</th>
<th>Example
</th>
<th>Description
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Conventional
</td>
<td>librarycatalog
</td>
<td>Search by keyword, title, author, etc.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Text-based
</td>
<td>Google, Bing, Yahoo!
</td>
<td>Search by keywords. Limited search using queries in natural language.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Voice_search" title="Voice search">Voice-based</a>
</td>
<td>Google, Bing, Yahoo!
</td>
<td>Search by keywords. Limited search using queries in natural language.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Multimedia_search" title="Multimedia search">Multimedia search</a>
</td>
<td>QBIC, WebSeek, SaFe
</td>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>

</td>
<td>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>There are basically three types of search engines: Those that are powered by robots (called <a href="/wiki/Web_crawler" title="Web crawler">crawlers</a>; ants or spiders), those that are powered by human submissions, and those that are a hybrid of the two.
</p><p>Crawler-based search engines are those that use automated software agents (called crawlers) that visit a Web site, read the information on the actual site, read the site's meta tags and also follow the links that the site connects to performing indexing on all linked Web sites as well. The crawler returns all that information back to a central depository, where the data is indexed. The crawler will periodically return to the sites to check for any information that has changed. The frequency with which this happens is determined by the administrators of the search engine.
</p><p>Human-powered search engines rely on humans to submit information that is subsequently indexed and catalogued. Only information that is submitted is put into the index.
</p><p>In both cases, when a user queries a search engine to locate information, they're actually searching through the index that the search engine has created —they are not actually searching the Web. These indices are giant databases of information that is collected and stored and subsequently searched. This explains why sometimes a search on a commercial search engine, such as Yahoo! or Google, will return results that are, in fact, dead links. Since the search results are based on the index, if the index has not been updated since a Web page became invalid the search engine treats the page as still an active link even though it no longer is. It will remain that way until the index is updated.
</p><p>So why will the same search on different search engines produce different results? Part of the answer to that question is because not all indices are going to be exactly the same. It depends on what the spiders find or what the humans submitted. But more important, not every search engine uses the same algorithm to search through the indices. The algorithm is what the search engines use to determine the <a href="/wiki/Relevance_(information_retrieval)" title="Relevance (information retrieval)">relevance</a> of the information in the index to what the user is searching for.
</p><p>One of the elements that a search engine algorithm scans for is the frequency and location of keywords on a Web page. Those with higher frequency are typically considered more relevant. But search engine technology is becoming sophisticated in its attempt to discourage what is known as keyword stuffing, or <a href="/wiki/Spamdexing" title="Spamdexing">spamdexing</a>.
</p><p>Another common element that algorithms analyze is the way that pages link to other pages in the Web. By analyzing how pages link to each other, an engine can both determine what a page is about (if the keywords of the linked pages are similar to the keywords on the original page) and whether that page is considered "important" and deserving of a boost in ranking. Just as the technology is becoming increasingly sophisticated to ignore keyword stuffing, it is also becoming more savvy to Web masters who build artificial links into their sites in order to build an artificial ranking.
</p><p>Modern web search engines are highly intricate software systems that employ technology that has evolved over the years. There are a number of sub-categories of search engine software that are separately applicable to specific 'browsing' needs. These include web search engines (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>), database or structured data search engines (e.g. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dieselpoint&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dieselpoint (page does not exist)">Dieselpoint</a>), and mixed search engines or enterprise search. The more prevalent search engines, such as Google and <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a>, utilize hundreds of thousands computers to process trillions of web pages in order to return fairly well-aimed results. Due to this high volume of queries and text processing, the software is required to run in a highly dispersed environment with a high degree of superfluity.
</p><p>Another category of search engines is scientific search engines. These are search engines which search scientific literature. The most prominent example is Google Scholar. Researchers are working on improving search engine technology by making them understand the content element of the articles, such as extracting theoretical constructs or key research findings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
